<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Whole genome sequencing of a banana wild relative Musa itinerans provides insights into lineage-specific diversification of the Musa genus"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2016 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-08-17"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Crop wild relatives are valuable resources for future genetic improvement. Here, we report the de novo genome assembly of Musa itinerans, a disease-resistant wild banana relative in subtropical China. The assembled genome size was 462.1&#8201;Mb, covering 75.2% of the genome (615.2Mb) and containing 32, 456 predicted protein-coding genes. Since the approximate divergence around 5.8 million years ago, the genomes of Musa itinerans and Musa acuminata have shown conserved collinearity. Gene family expansions and contractions enrichment analysis revealed that some pathways were associated with phenotypic or physiological innovations. These include a transition from wood to herbaceous in the ancestral Musaceae, intensification of cold and drought tolerances and reduced diseases resistance genes for subtropical marginally distributed Musa species. Prevalent purifying selection and transposed duplications were found to facilitate the diversification of NBS-encoding gene families for two Musa species. The population genome history analysis of M. itinerans revealed that the fluctuated population sizes were caused by the Pleistocene climate oscillations and that the formation of Qiongzhou Strait might facilitate the population downsizing on the isolated Hainan Island about 10.3 Kya. The qualified assembly of the M. itinerans genome provides deep insights into the lineage-specific diversification and also valuable resources for future banana breeding."/>

        <ul class="c-article-subject-list">

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Crop wild relatives are valuable resources for future genetic improvement. Here, we report the <i>de novo</i> genome assembly of <i>Musa itinerans</i>, a disease-resistant wild banana relative in subtropical China. The assembled genome size was 462.1 Mb, covering 75.2% of the genome (615.2Mb) and containing 32, 456 predicted protein-coding genes. Since the approximate divergence around 5.8 million years ago, the genomes of <i>Musa itinerans</i> and <i>Musa acuminata</i> have shown conserved collinearity. Gene family expansions and contractions enrichment analysis revealed that some pathways were associated with phenotypic or physiological innovations. These include a transition from wood to herbaceous in the ancestral Musaceae, intensification of cold and drought tolerances and reduced diseases resistance genes for subtropical marginally distributed <i>Musa</i> species. Prevalent purifying selection and transposed duplications were found to facilitate the diversification of NBS-encoding gene families for two <i>Musa</i> species. The population genome history analysis of <i>M. itinerans</i> revealed that the fluctuated population sizes were caused by the Pleistocene climate oscillations and that the formation of Qiongzhou Strait might facilitate the population downsizing on the isolated Hainan Island about 10.3 Kya. The qualified assembly of the <i>M. itinerans</i> genome provides deep insights into the lineage-specific diversification and also valuable resources for future banana breeding.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">
                        
                            
    
    <div class="c-pdf-download u-clear-both">
        <a href="/articles/srep31586.pdf" class="c-pdf-download__link" data-article-pdf="true" data-readcube-pdf-url="true" data-test="download-pdf" data-draft-ignore="true" data-track="click" data-track-action="download pdf" data-track-label="link" data-track-external download>
            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Crop wild relatives (CWRs) are composed of progenitors and more or less distantly related species of crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Hajjar, R. &amp; Hodgkin, T. The use of wild relatives in crop improvement: a survey of developments over the last 20 years. Euphytica 156, 1–13 (2007)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6416e519">1</a></sup>. They were gene pools for many desirable traits including yields, nutrition quality, resistances to pests and diseases and abiotic tolerances to drought, salt, cold etc. Due to substantial loss of genetic variations during domestication, several domestication syndromes such as disease susceptibility were common to many crop species. To help cope with these challenges, plant breeders have resorted to CWRs and made significant advances in the past 30 years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Heslop-Harrison, J. S. &amp; Schwarzacher, T. Domestication, genomics and the future for banana. Ann. Bot. 100, 1073–1084 (2007)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d6416e523">2</a></sup>. However, due to climate change and human-mediated disturbances, many of the CWRs were threatened and poorly conserved, so it’s imperative to take immediate action to collect, conserve and evaluate them<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ford-Lloyd, B. V. et al. Crop Wild Relatives—Undervalued, Underutilized and under Threat? Bioscience 61, 559–565 (2011)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d6416e527">3</a></sup>. In recent years, the coined term ‘super-domestication’ referred to the process of obtaining target traits by manipulation on crops or CWRs via genetic and genomic approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Vaughan, D. A., Balazs, E. &amp; Heslop-Harrison, J. S. From Crop Domestication to Super-domestication. Ann. Bot. 100, 893–901 (2007)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d6416e531">4</a></sup>. Thus, genome sequencing of CWRs will facilitate the discovery of useful alleles or key genomic elements that can contribute to the improvement of targeted traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Brozynska, M., Furtado, A. &amp; Henry, R. J. Genomics of crop wild relatives: expanding the gene pool for crop improvement. Plant Biotechnol. J. 14, 1070–1085 (2016)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d6416e535">5</a></sup>. Banana is one of the most favoured fruits worldwide and also an important staple food for people in some African and Latin American countries. Bananas are diploid or triploid hybrids between <i>Musa acuminata</i> (A genome) and <i>M. balbisiana</i> (B genome) or hybrids between subspecies of <i>M. acuminata</i>. Originally, domesticated in Southeast Asia about 7000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Denham, T. P. et al. Origins of agriculture at Kuk Swamp in the highlands of New Guinea. Science 301, 189–193 (2003)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6416e549">6</a></sup>, the seedless and parthenocarpic hybrids have been thereafter widely propagated by vegetative reproduction. Long-standing human selections on these spontaneous triploids or diploids have contributed to the diversity of cultivated bananas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Perrier, X. et al. Multidisciplinary perspectives on banana (Musa spp.) domestication. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 11311–11318 (2011)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6416e553">7</a></sup>. Today, one triploid subgroup (AAA genome group), namely Cavendish that originated from a single clone, contributes to almost half of the world’s banana production. However, an emerging strain of <i>Fusarium</i> wilt disease, Tropical Race 4′ (<i>Fusarium oxysporum</i> f. sp. <i>cubense</i> race 4, Foc-TR4) has caused devastating damages to this cultivar. There are about 60 species in the <i>Musa</i> genus and some of them have been utilized in banana resistance improvement, for instance, a wild non-edible accession of <i>M. acuminata</i> ssp. <i>burmannica</i> was once extensively used in banana breeding for its resistances to black Sigatoka and some races of <i>Fusarium</i> wilt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Heslop-Harrison, J. S. &amp; Schwarzacher, T. Domestication, genomics and the future for banana. Ann. Bot. 100, 1073–1084 (2007)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d6416e580">2</a></sup>. Therefore, wild relatives potentially harbour beneficial alleles for cultivated crop and are very important resources for future banana breeding strategies.</p><p>In 2012, the genome of <i>M. acuminata</i> ssp <i>malaccensis</i> (A genome), one progenitor of the cultivated banana, was sequenced and the genome evolution peculiarities of the Musaceae in the monocots were thus revealed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6416e593">8</a></sup>. Recently, the draft genome of the other progenitor of banana, <i>M. balbisiana</i> (B genome) has been released<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Davey, M. W. et al. A draft Musa balbisiana genome sequence for molecular genetics in polyploid, inter- and intra-specific Musa hybrids. BMC Genomics 14, 683 (2013)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d6416e600">9</a></sup>. With the ever-decreasing costs of genome sequencing, it is becoming routine to sequence more genomes even within a genus, which can elucidate lineage-specific diversification mechanisms and genome diversities, as the case of rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, Q.-J. et al. Rapid diversification of five Oryza AA genomes associated with rice adaptation. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. 111, E4954–E4962 (2014)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6416e605">10</a></sup>. Being one of the most valued crop species worldwide, the additional genome resources of banana are required to reveal hidden genetic diversity for future crop improvements. In this study, we contributed the draft genome sequence for <i>M. itinerans,</i> a close relative to both banana progenitors with wide distribution across subtropical China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, L. F., Häkkinen, M., Yuan, Y. M., Hao, G. &amp; Ge, X. J. Molecular phylogeny and systematics of the banana family (Musaceae) inferred from multiple nuclear and chloroplast DNA fragments, with a special reference to the genus Musa. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 57, 1–10 (2010)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d6416e612">11</a></sup>. <i>Musa itinerans var itinerans</i>, known also as Yunnan banana, is native to south-east Asia and can be found in moist ravines to mountainous areas up to 2200 m<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Häkkinen, M., Wang, H. &amp; Ge, Xue-jun . Musa itinerans(Musaceae) and its intraspecific taxa in China. Nord. J. Bot. 26, 317–324 (2008)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6416e619">12</a></sup>. In China, it is usually found in secondary tropical rainforests. It grows fast and can produce long rhizome with sucker emerging more than 2 meters from the mother plant, explaining its name origin. Interestingly, it was shown as one of the most Foc-TR4 resistant and cold tolerant species in the <i>Musa</i> genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Li, W. M. et al. Resistance sources to Fusarium oxysporum f. sp. cubense tropical race 4 in banana wild relatives. Plant Pathol. 64, 1061–1067 (2015)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6416e627">13</a></sup>, providing valuable resource for the disease resistance and hardiness improvement in banana breeding. <i>M. itinerans</i> also displays interesting agronomic characteristic such as rapid fruit maturation (personal observation, Häkkinen M). Moreover, the studies will facilitate the understanding of the lineage-specific genome evolution in the <i>Musa</i> genus.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and annotation</h3><p>One individual plant of <i>M. itinerans</i> collected from Baoting County on Hainan Island and transplanted in South China Botanical Garden was sequenced by whole-genome shotgun strategy on the Illumina Hiseq2000 platform (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1, Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). A total of 74.2 Gb clean reads (approximately 120.7 fold coverage) derived from five libraries with insertion size ranging from 170 bp to 10 kb were used (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1, Supplementary Note 2</a>). Hierarchical assembly implemented in SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6416e658">14</a></sup> yielded a draft genome of 462.1 Mb, representing 75.2% of the estimated genome size (615.2 Mb) based on a 17-mer statistics (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2, Supplementary Table 2, Supplementary Note 3, 4</a>). In total, the assembly comprised 7194 scaffolds (above 2 kb) with N50 of 192 kb and 103,534 contigs (above 100 bp) with N50 of 33.9 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3</a>). The post-assembly quality evaluations showed that the distribution of GC content was concentrated around 0.388, well in accord with that of the published genome <i>M. acuminata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6416e674">8</a></sup> and the average base coverage was about 92× (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs 3 and 4</a>). The total heterozygous rate was estimated to be 0.25% based on the ratio of the number of single-nucleotide polymorphisms to the overall genome length, after excluding ‘N’ sites of assembly gaps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 4</a>). Using the package CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: A pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d6416e685">15</a></sup>, the complete and partial coverage of core eukaryotic gene sets (CEGMA) for the <i>M. itinerans</i> genome assembly were 79.8% and 94.4% respectively, which were very close to the <i>M. acuminata</i> genome assembly (81.9% and 93.6% respectively) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Thus, we obtained a qualified draft genome assembly, which should be valuable genomic resources to support banana breeding.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the assembly of the <i>Musa itinerans</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep31586/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using both homology-search against the RepBase library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d6416e937">16</a></sup> and <i>de novo</i> predictions with RepeatModeler approaches, 38.95% (179.99 Mbp) of the <i>Musa itinerans</i> genome were predicted to contain repetitive elements, somewhat higher than that of <i>M. acuminata</i> (35.43%, 167.59 Mbp) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>) and the higher level of repetitive elements for <i>M. itinerans</i> might be caused by using heterozygous diploids instead of the double haploid used for <i>M. acuminata.</i> Of the most abundant retrotransposons, long-terminal repeats (LTRs) were predominant in <i>M. itinerans</i> and the two most common subfamilies LTR/Copia (16.7%) and LTR/Gypsy (16.3%) were almost in equal proportions in <i>M. itinerans</i>. The two peaks around sequence divergence rate were 20% and 30%, indicating that LTRs families in <i>M. itinerans</i> underwent two rounds of expansion events (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Using the intact 5′- and 3′- LTR sequences of the retrotransposons within the genome, the insertion time of these LTRs for both <i>Musa</i> species were estimated and quite large proportions of them burst after the divergence between <i>M. itinerans</i> and <i>M. acuminata</i> (approximately 5.8 million years ago (Mya), estimated by this study), suggestive of high turnover rates of retrotransposable elements in the <i>Musa</i> genomes. One recent burst of LTRs occurring at about 0.2 Mya in <i>M. itinerans</i> but not in <i>M. acuminata</i>, showed rapid dynamic evolution for these repeat elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). For <i>M. itinerans</i>, only 3.15% of the genome was the class II elements and the compositions resembled those of <i>M. acuminata</i>, in which CACTA and Mariner, the most common superfamilies in other plants were absent. Instead, two super-families CMC (0.97%) and hAT (0.90%) were the most common types (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Using the protein sequences of six species (<i>M. acuminata</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Zea mays</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>) as queries against the repeat-masked assemblies, 32,456 protein-coding genes were predicted with an average coding sequence length of 1065 bp and 5.2 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 6</a>). For these predicted genes, 86.9% of them were annotated with the SWISS-PROT, KEGG, GO, InterPro and TrEMBL databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 7</a>). In addition to protein-coding genes, other non-coding RNA including 345 microRNA, 977 tRNA, 278 rRNA and 299 snRNA were also identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome evolution</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec5">Gene family expansions and contractions</h4><p>We conducted protein-based clustering with OrthoMCL among <i>M. itinerans</i> and other eight species, namely, <i>M. acuminata</i>, <i>M. balbisiana</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>, <i>Elaeis guineensis</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor, Vitis vinifera,</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana.</i> Of the 28,524 predicted protein sequences (&gt;100 amino-acids in length) in <i>M. itinerans</i>, 26,955 (94.5%) were assigned into 16,742 gene families with 1201 single-copy orthologs and 177 gene families were found to be unique to <i>M. itinerans</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9, Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). A 5-way comparison of the three <i>Musa</i> species and two closely related Arecaceae species <i>P. dactylifera,</i> and <i>E. guineensis</i> showed that 7810 gene families were shared among them and 2723 uniquely shared gene families between the three <i>Musa</i> genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). <i>M. itinerans</i> shared more gene families with <i>M. auminata</i> (2727 gene families) than with <i>M. balbisiana</i> (140 gene families), which were suggestive of higher similarity for the latter two species. The unique gene families of <i>M. itinerans</i> among the five species were significantly enriched for 40 GO terms and involved in some essential functional categories such as DNA polymerase activity (GO: 0034061, P = 1.56e-9, hyper-geometric test), endoribonuclease activity (GO: 0016891, P = 5.12e-9, hyper-geometric test) and ribonuclease activity (GO:0004540, P = 4.15e-7, hyper-geometric test) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Twelve genes were significantly mapped on the ribosome pathway (ko03010, P = 4.64e-5, hyper-geometric test) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 11</a>) and it has been suggested that ribosomes might play some roles in the intrinsic regulation of a subset of mRNAs translation with unique cis-regulatory elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Xue, S. &amp; Barna, M. Specialized ribosomes: a new frontier in gene regulation and organismal biology. Nat. Rev. Mol. Cell Biol. 13, 355–369 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1114">17</a></sup>. The followed proteasome pathway (ko03050, P = 1.77e-5, hyper-geometric test) involved the degradation of abnormal or damaged proteins in the cell and played crucial roles in response to diverse intracellular signals and changing environmental conditions such infection, heat and cold etc<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Sullivan, J. a., Shirasu, K. &amp; Deng, X. W. The diverse roles of ubiquitin and the 26S proteasome in the life of plants. Nat. Rev. Genet. 4, 948–958 (2003)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1119">18</a></sup>. Other enriched pathways fell into diverse categories with development process, growth, response to biotic or abiotic stresses, such as plant hormone signal transduction (ko04075), inositol phosphate metabolism (ko00562), fatty acid biosynthesis (ko00061), plant-pathogen interaction (ko04626) and starch and sucrose metabolism (ko00500) etc. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S11</a>). Overall, these unique gene families of <i>M. itinerans</i> might also give some hints on how a subtropical marginally distributed species coped with the challenging environmental conditions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep31586/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep31586/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep31586_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep31586/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep31586_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="872" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Gene family relationships between <i>Musa itinerans</i> and other sequenced genomes.</p><p>(<b>a</b>) The Venn diagram represents shared/unique orthologous gene families between <i>Musa itinerans</i> and other three species (<i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>Musa balbisiana</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>); (<b>b</b>) Gene family expansions or contractions in three <i>Musa</i> genomes. Numbers to the right and left of the slash were expanded (+) and contracted (−) gene families, respectively. Red and blue pie-charts indicate the proportions of changed gene families and background gene family sets, respectively. Maximum likelihood tree was constructed using four-fold degenerate transversion sites of 1201 single-copy gene families,and the values at the node indicated the estimates of divergence time (MYA) with a 95% credibility interval.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep31586/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparison of orthologous gene families among nine related species revealed that 2870 expanded and 1032 contracted gene families in the ancestor of the three <i>Musa</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). The expanded gene families were enriched in 29 pathways, of which the most striking expansions were associated with phagosome (ko04145, hyper-geometric test, P = 2.18e-20) and plant-pathogen interaction (ko04626, hyper-geometric test, P = 1.11e-19) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 12</a>). Phagosome biogenesis was a key process in tissue remodeling, clearing apoptotic cells and restricting the spread of intracellular pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Parniske, M. Intracellular accommodation of microbes by plants: A common developmental program for symbiosis and disease? Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 3, 320–328 (2000)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1187">19</a></sup>, together with the plant-pathogen interaction pathway (ko04626), both of which provided some clues on how tropical emergent taxa coped with the intrinsic or exogenous biotic stresses. Besides these two important functional categories, the remaining 27 pathways involved in many other important component biogenesis processes and might be associated with some phenotypic or physiological innovations in the ancestral <i>Musa</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 12</a>). In contrast, the ancestral <i>Musa</i> species lost fewer gene families and only 15 pathways were significantly over-represented (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13</a>). One noteworthy pathway, phenylalanine metabolism (ko00360, P = 3.44e-05, hyper-geometric test) was considered to be a key step in the lignin biosynthesis of vascular plants and the contraction of this pathway might perturb the lignin biosynthesis and cell-wall architecture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Anderson, N. A. &amp; Chapple, C. Perturbing Lignin Biosynthesis: Metabolic Changes in Response to Manipulation of the Phenylpropanoid Pathway. Recent Adv. Polyphen. Res. 39–59 (2014)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1204">20</a></sup>, thus might result in the crucial transition from a woody to herbaceous disposition in the ancestors of <i>Musa</i> species. As one of the most marginally distributed <i>Musa</i> species in subtropical areas, 21 expanded and 24 contracted pathways were enriched for <i>M. itinerans</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 14 and15</a>), some of which were in accordance with the release from biotic-stresses such as pathogens (ABC transporter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kang, J. et al. Plant ABC Transporters. Arabidopsis Book 9, e0153 (2011)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1221">21</a></sup>, ko02010, P = 1.25e-17, hyper-geometric test; plant-pathogen interaction, ko04626, P = 2.28e-9, hyper-geometric test) and intensification of abiotic stresses such as drought and cold (phagosome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Parniske, M. Intracellular accommodation of microbes by plants: A common developmental program for symbiosis and disease? Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 3, 320–328 (2000)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1225">19</a></sup>, ko04145, P = 6.61e-24, hyper-geometric test; amino sugar and nucleotide sugar metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Vinocur, B. &amp; Altman, A. Recent advances in engineering plant tolerance to abiotic stress: Achievements and limitations. Curr. Opin. Biotechnol. 16, 123–132 (2005)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1229">22</a></sup>, ko00520, P = 8.13e-13, hyper-geometric test).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec6">Genome duplication and divergence</h4><p>According to a calibrated divergence time of 187.9 Mya (95% confidence interval:124.0~248.4 Mya) between <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>sorghum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Clarke, J. T., Warnock, R. C. M. &amp; Donoghue, P. C. J. Establishing a time-scale for plant evolution. New Phytol. 192, 266–301 (2011)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1246">23</a></sup>, the divergence time between <i>M. itinerans and M. acuminata</i> was estimated to be 5.8 Mya (95% confidence interval: 3.4~9.0 Mya) based on four-fold degenerated sites (4DTv sites) of 1201 single-copy orthologs and the ancestor of <i>M. itinerans and M. acuminata</i> diverged with <i>M. balbisiana</i> about 8.3 Mya (95% confidence interval: 4.8~13.1 Mya) and Musaceae and Arecaceae coalesced to 120.9 Mya (95% confidence interval 73.4~181.2 Mya) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>).</p><p>Using SynMap, a web-based tool on the plant comparative genome platform CoGe (<a href="https://genomevolution.org/CoGe/">https://genomevolution.org/CoGe/</a>), syntenic regions between <i>M. itinerans</i> and <i>M. acuminata</i> were generated. Based on the collinear blocks between <i>M. itinerans</i> and <i>M. acuminata</i>, 26,670 genes, comprising of 82.5% of the <i>M. itinerans</i> gene sets distributed on 1043 scaffolds, were oriented and anchored to the 11 linkage groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 9, Supplementary Table 16</a>). The global view of the genome <i>M. itinerans</i> including gene density, GC content distribution, repeat elements distribution were also plotted along the pseudo-chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>) and it showed that these genes unevenly distributed among given window slides (P = 0, Chi-square test) and positively correlated with the GC content significantly (P = 2.2e-16, Spear’s rank correlation rho = 0.35), as predicted by GC content and gene density<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Glémin, S., Clément, Y., David, J. &amp; Ressayre, A. GC content evolution in coding regions of angiosperm genomes: A unifying hypothesis. Trends Genet. 30, 263–270 (2014)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1301">24</a></sup>. Using the MCScanX Program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: A toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, 1–11 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1305">25</a></sup>, 510 syntenic blocks covered 36.25% of gene repertoire of <i>M. itinerans</i> were identified, with average number of 13, ranging from 6 to 164 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Fig2">Fig. 2f</a>); and 695 syntenic blocks composed of 36.10% of the final gene set in <i>M. acuminata</i> were found (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Based on the distributions of synonymous substitutions rate (<i>Ks</i>) of these syntenic orthologous and paralogous gene pairs, the speciation and paleoploidization events in the <i>Musa</i> genus were inferred. When following an average <i>Ks</i> of 3.47 per 10<sup>9</sup> years for nuclear genes in the Zingiberales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1334">8</a></sup>, <i>M. itinerans</i> and <i>M. acuminata</i> diverged approximately about 5.8 million years ago with peak <i>Ks</i> around 0.04 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). This divergence time estimation was consistent with that based on the phylogeny of 1201 single copy nuclear genes among nine plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). For both <i>Musa</i> species, one peak around <i>K</i><sub><i>s</i></sub> 0.40~0.42 for intragenomic syntenic paralogs indicated an ancient genome duplication event occurred about 57.6~61.0 Mya, which was consistent with the polyploidization explosions in many plant species around Cretaceous-Tertiary boundary<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Van de Peer, Y., Fawcett, J. A., Proost, S., Sterck, L. &amp; Vandepoele, K. The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 680–688 (2009)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1367">26</a></sup>. According to the ancestral syntenic blocks inferences in <i>Musa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1373">8</a></sup>, this genome duplication should correspond to the two in separate successive duplication events (α/β) in <i>M. acuminata.</i></p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep31586/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep31586/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep31586_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep31586/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep31586_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="686" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The global view of <i>Musa itinerans</i> genome.</p><p>Pseudo-chromosomes of <i>M. itinerans</i> were constructed by anchoring and orienting the scaffolds of <i>M. itinerans</i> among the chromosomes of <i>Musa acuminata</i> based on the collinearity between them. Concentric circles were shown by window size of 100 kb. Tracks displayed were: (a) gene density, min = 1, max = 29; (b) GC content ratio, min = 0.3, max = 0.5; (c) all repeat content ratio, min = 0, max = 0.9; (d) LTR/Copia content ratio, min = 0,max = 0.6; (e) LTR/Gyspy content ratio, min = 0, max = 0.5; (f) intragenomic collinearity.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep31586/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp"
