speciation events among <i>Musa itinerans</i> and <i>Musa acuminata</i>.</p><p>The distributions of 4DTv (transversions at fourfold degenerate sites) distances between orthologs and the distributions of 4DTv between paralogs within genomes were used to infer the possible speciation events and whole-genome duplication events, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep31586/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evolution of NBS and MYB gene families</h3><p>Following the method described in D’Hont <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1449">8</a></sup>, 62, 93, 117 NBS-encoding genes were identified in the genomes of <i>M. itinerans, M. balbisiana, M. acuminata,</i> respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 17</a>). The decreasing number of NBS genes was consistent with the latitudinal ascending of the three species. During the transition from humid tropical to cool subtropical habitats, some NBS related genes would be less numerous due to relaxed selection constraints on them. The absence of Toll/interleukin-1 receptor (TIR)-like (TIR-NBS types) in the three <i>Musa</i> species was consistent with previous reports in monocots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Peraza-Echeverria, S., Dale, J. L., Harding, R. M., Smith, M. K. &amp; Collet, C. Characterization of disease resistance gene candidates of the nucleotide binding site (NBS) type from banana and correlation of a transcriptional polymorphism with resistance to Fusarium oxysporum f.sp. Ceubense race 4. Mol. Breed 22, 565–579 (2008)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1462">27</a></sup> and these NBS genes were distributed unevenly among the 11 chromosomes with skewed distributions on chromosome 3, 6, 9, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). A large proportion of the NBS-encoding genes were singletons and only a few of them clustered into gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 17, Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). The predominant transposed duplication mode for the NBS-encoding genes might contribute to the widespread singletons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 18 and 19</a>), which was prone to cause drastic gene structural divergence and function diversification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, Y., Tan, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Different patterns of gene structure divergence following gene duplication in Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics 14, 652 (2013)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1476">28</a></sup>. Transposed duplication modes were found to be responsible for the proliferation of other important gene families including MADS-box, F-box, B3 transcription factors in Brassicales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Freeling, M. et al. Many or most genes in Arabidopsis transposed after the origin of the order Brassicales. Genome Res. 18, 1924–1937 (2008)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1480">29</a></sup>. In addition, transposed duplications were observed to span wider time scales than other duplication modes in both <i>Musa</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). With duplication ages in terms of <i>Ks</i> ranged from 0.01 to 5.50 with average 1.59 for <i>M. itinerans</i> and from 0.02 to 3.93 with average 0.83 for <i>M. acuminata</i>, vast majority of the NBS-encoding genes were duplicated well prior to the divergence of <i>M. itinerans</i> and <i>M. acuminata</i> (<i>Ks</i> = 0.04), hence, the ancient ages of these duplicated NBS-encoding genes were not in accordance with the classic arm-race model, which predicted a high turnover rate and predominant young NBS-encoding genes within a genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Holub, E. B. The arms race is ancient history in Arabidopsis, the wildflower. Nat. Rev. Genet. 2, 516–527 (2001)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1510">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bergelson, J., Kreitman, M., Stahl, E. a. &amp; Tian, D. Evolutionary dynamics of plant R-genes. Science (80-). 292, 2281–2285 (2001)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1513">31</a></sup>. <i>K</i><sub><i>a</i></sub>/<i>K</i><sub><i>s</i></sub> (nonsynonymous to synonymous substitution ratio) values of most duplicated pairs (segmental duplication, tandem duplication, proximate duplication, transposed duplication) within both genomes were less than 1, indicating that purifying selection played an essential role in the maintenance of intra-genomic diversity of these NBS-encoding genes in <i>Musa</i>, as the cases in chestnut<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Zhong, Y., Li, Y., Huang, K. &amp; Cheng, Z.-M. Species-specific duplications of NBS-encoding genes in Chinese chestnut (Castanea mollissima). Sci. Rep. 5, 16638 (2015)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1533">32</a></sup>, sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Mace, E. et al. The plasticity of NBS resistance genes in sorghum is driven by multiple evolutionary processes. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 253 (2014)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1537">33</a></sup>, <i>Solanum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Couch, B. C., Spangler, R., Ramos, C. &amp; May, G. Pervasive purifying selection characterizes the evolution of I2 homologs. Mol. Plant. Microbe. Interact. 19, 288–303 (2006)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1543">34</a></sup>. In our comparative studies in <i>Musa</i>, the NBS-encoding gene repertoire seemed to be associated with their specific habitat shift.</p><p>Transcription factor (TF) genes for the two <i>Musa</i> species were identified by blast against known transcription factor gene families of related species, as well as homology prediction using all the known transcription factor proteins to blast against the <i>M. itinerans</i> genome. In total, 3176 and 2898 putative transcription factor genes, from 58 families, were identified for <i>M. itinerans</i> and <i>M. acuminata</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 20</a>). These numbers represent 9.8% and 7.9% of the 32,456 and 36,542 predicted protein-coding loci. Among the 58 transcription factor families identified, MYB, AP2, Dof, G2-like, SBP, HD-zip, WOX, RAV, LBD and SAP were over-represented in both <i>Musa</i> species relative to other taxa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Similarly to <i>M. acuminata</i>, the <i>M. itinerans</i> genome contains a large number of TFs that can be explained by the 3 rounds of specific WGD in the Zingiberales order<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1581">8</a></sup>.</p><p>The MYB family was the largest transcription factor family in the two <i>Musa</i> species. With the number of repeats in the N-terminal conserved MYB DNA-binding domain, the MYB super-family can been categorized into four classes, 1R-, R2R3-, 3R- and 4R-MYB and the largest one R2R3 MYB was plant specific and key regulators in metabolism and responses to biotic and abiotic stress, also in flowering or fruit development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Dubos, C. et al. MYB transcription factors in Arabidopsis. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 573–581 (2010)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1591">35</a></sup>. About 292 and 304 <i>MYB</i> genes were identified in the genomes of <i>M. acuminata</i> and <i>M. itinerans</i> after manual inspection, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 21, Supplementary Fig. 15</a>) and 92.1% and 93.4% of them were R2R3-type <i>MYB genes</i> and much higher than in Arabidopsis (56.8%) and rice (70.1%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Katiyar, A. et al. Genome-wide classification and expression analysis of MYB transcription factor families in rice and Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics 13, 544 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1611">36</a></sup>. The extensive collinearity between <i>M. itinerans</i> and <i>M. acuminata</i> for these MYB gene pairs was observed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 16a</a>). The intragenomic duplicated MYB gene pairs also showed good synteny and relatively even distribution across chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 16b,c</a>). For <i>M. acuminata</i>, this gene family arose primarily by segmental duplications (about 186 gene pairs) with median Ks = 0.57 and followed by transposed duplications with median Ks = 0.89 (about 97 gene pairs), both of which were prior to the ancient α/β whole genome duplication events (Ks = 0.40~0.42) in Musaceae (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 22</a>). The MYB TFs’ gene families are essential to plant morphogenesis and diverse physiological processes and the gene balance hypothesis assumed that these regulating gene families were dosage sensitive and robust to gene loss after whole genome duplication events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Birchler, J. A., Riddle, N. C., Auger, D. L. &amp; Veitia, R. A. Dosage balance in gene regulation: Biological implications. Trends Genet. 21, 219–226 (2005)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1634">37</a></sup>.About 89.7% of these segmental duplicated gene pairs were under purifying selection (with <i>K</i><sub><i>a</i></sub>/<i>K</i><sub><i>s</i></sub> &lt; 1), followed by transposed duplications (about 97 gene pairs, 84.6% of them with <i>K</i><sub><i>a</i></sub>/<i>K</i><sub><i>s</i></sub> &lt; 1). Under this scenario, purifying selections were expected to wipe out deleterious mutations and stabilize the MYB TFs gene repertoire for their essential functions. Similar evolutionary patterns were observed in <i>M. itinerans</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 23</a>), indicating that the conservative evolutionary pattern of MYB transcription factor families in the Musaceae family.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Demographic history of <i>M. itinerans</i></h3><p>To infer the demographic history of <i>M. itinerans,</i> each individual from three populations along different latitudes were sampled and re-sequenced in high depth (HN: 71.0-fold coverage; LC: 48.4-fold coverage; YC: 54.8-fold coverage) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Note1</a>). Using the pairwise sequentially Markovian coalescent (PSMC) model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Inference of human population history from individual whole-genome sequences. Nature 475, 493–496 (2011)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1687">38</a></sup>, the population size changes of the three populations were inferred respectively. The PSMC model estimated the coalescent time distributions between two alleles across all chromosomes based on the density of heterozygous sites across the diploid genome of a single individual, which in turn can be transformed into effective population size (<i>N</i><sub><i>e</i></sub>) since the inverse relationship between the effective population size and coalescent rate. The population history analysis of each individual from one population depicted the nearly identical trajectory (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). Using a generation time of one year and mutation rate 1.30 × 10<sup>−8</sup> substitution rate per site per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1703">39</a></sup>, the genomic variations of the three populations coalesced between 3Ma and 300 kya. Since approximately 150 Kya, the <i>N</i><sub><i>e</i></sub> of the marginal ancestral HN population (HN population, Hainan, China) was substantial less than either of the two central populations (LC, Lechang population, northern Guangdong province, China; YC, Yangchun population. southern Guangdong, China) and this spatial pattern of genetic variations was consistent with theoretical prediction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Antonovics, J., McKane, A. J. &amp; Newman, T. J. Spatiotemporal dynamics in marginal populations. Am. Nat. 167, 16–27 (2006)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1713">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Mandák, B., Bímová, K., Plačková, I., Mahelka, V. &amp; Chrtek, J. Loss of genetic variation in geographically marginal populations of Atriplex tatarica (Chenopodiaceae). Ann. Bot. 96, 901–912 (2005)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1716">41</a></sup>. The effective population size of the three populations increased since 300 kya and lasted until 70 kya for the HN population and about 20 kya for the two continental populations. During the Pleistocene cycles of glacial and interglacial episodes, frequent population bottlenecks and subsequent expansions were expected to reduce the effective population sizes, however, population migrations and concomitant admixtures during expansions among highly heterogeneous populations might lead to increased <i>N</i><sub><i>e</i></sub><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wang, J. &amp; Whitlock, M. C. Estimating Effective Population Size and Migration Rates From Genetic Samples Over Space and Time. Genetics 163, 429–446 (2003)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1726">42</a></sup> on the other hand, the heterogeneous topography in South China would result in the persistent population substructure, which was also possible for the elevated Ne during the glacial and interglacial cycles. It’s possible that the counter effect of admixtures between populations of <i>M. itinerans</i> overrode that of the population bottleneck until the progressive disappearance of population heterogeneities at the Last Glacial Maximum (LGM, c.a 18–20 Kya). However, due to genetic deficiency or less frequent admixtures, the isolated HN population showed more rapid decay of heterogeneity and earlier shrinkage of Ne than either of the continental populations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). The final formation of the Qiongzhou Strait was in the Middle Holocene when marine strata transgressed the original lowland and the Hainan Island was completely isolated from Leizhou Peninsula<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Zhao, H., Wang, L. &amp; Yuan, J. Origin and time of Qiongzhou Strait. Mar. Geol. Quat. Geol. 27, 33–39 (2007)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1736">43</a></sup>, so an additional Ne reduction of the HN Island population was observed at the time 10.3 Kya.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep31586/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep31586/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep31586_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep31586/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep31586_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="243" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Effective population size changes of <i>Musa itinerans</i> over time using the Pairwise Sequentially Markovian Coalescent (PSMC) model.</p><p>Using a generation time (g) of one year per generation and a mutation rate of 1.30 × 10<sup>−8</sup> substitutions per generation, the PSMC results were scaled to real time. Three different geographic populations were marked by distinctive color curves and the most drastic population size change was shadowed. As shown, the effective population sizes of the two continental populations (YC, LC) were downsized in closely approximate epoch and more recent than the island population (HN).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep31586/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Genome sequencing of the wild banana <i>Musa itinerans,</i> one of the most disease-resistant wild banana species, provides invaluable genetic resources for future banana genetic improvement. The <i>M. itinerans</i> diverged from <i>M. acuminata</i> only 5.8 Mya and exhibited extensive collinearity between them, but the two species occupy distinct habitats, which provides an unpreceded chance to unravel the diversification within the <i>Musa</i> genus. Resorting to lineage-specific gene family expansions and contractions approaches, a batch of candidate genes categorized into diverse pathways or GO terms were inferred to be associated with some peculiar traits. However, more delicate experiments were required to clarify their roles in the physiological or phenotypic innovations, as one of the most disease resistant and cold hardy species in the <i>Musa</i> genus, the gene repertoire space of <i>M. itinerans</i> should be mined in the future. The non-synonymous to synonymous ratio <i>K</i><sub><i>a</i></sub>/<i>K</i><sub><i>s</i></sub> of &lt;1 for most NBS-encoding gene family revealed pervasive purifying selections, together with the ancient duplication ages, both in support of the trench-warfare model hypothesis and against the classic arms-race model. A similar evolution pattern was also observed in the MYB transcription factor family, in which the purifying selection wiped out deleterious mutations and stabilize the MYB TFs’ gene repertoire for their essential functions. Demographic history has a profound effect on the genetic diversity of extant species. The isolated HN population of <i>M. itinerans</i> was more prone to genetic loss during glacial and interglacial cycles than the counterpart mainland populations, in which admixture differentially buffered against genetic loss. Furthermore, the detailed scenarios of demographic history for this marginally distributed species will be revealed with more extensive sampling and population genome resequencing studies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Sampling preparation and Sequencing</h3><p>Natural populations of <i>M. itinerans</i> are distributed in subtropical China and individual leaves of <i>M. itinerans</i> were harvested and immediately storied with sillica-gel in the field (the source of material see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes 1</a>). The total genomic DNA was extracted with the CTAB (cetyl trimethylammonium bromide) method and qualified with A260/280 ratio and agarose gel electrophoresis for further use. To obtain a high-quality reference genome, we sequenced one individual of <i>M. itinerans</i> from Hainan population using a whole-genome shotgun sequencing strategy. Paired and mate-pair Illumina genomic DNA libraries with varying insert size (180 bp, 500 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb and 10 kb) were constructed following the manufacturer’s instructions. All the libraries were sequenced on the Illumina Hiseq2000 platform. Raw reads were subject to the removals of PCR duplicates, adaptor sequences and contaminants of bacterial or viruses. The processed high-quality reads were assembled into contigs and scaffolds using SOAPdenovo2 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1833">14</a></sup> and gaps were filled using Gapcloser (version 1.12, <a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html</a>). The reference genome size of <i>M. itinerans</i> was estimated based on the K-mer frequency distribution analysis (details for <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2</a>). Two individuals each from the YC and LC populations were sampled and sequenced using a paired-end library size of 500 bp with &gt;30-fold coverage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome annotation</h3><p>For the draft genome of <i>M. itinerans</i>, interspersed repeats were identified using RepeatMasker v3.30 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) and homolog research for the Repbase database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1869">16</a></sup>. Repeat proteins were characterized using RepeatProteinMask v3.30 with default parameters, <i>de novo</i> interspersed repeats were annotated using RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>). Tandem repeats in the genome of <i>M. itinerans</i> were screened using Tandem Repeat Finder v4.04<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: A program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1887">44</a></sup>. Next, a homology searching approach was used to identify protein-coding genes. We obtained the protein sequences of <i>Musa acuminata</i> v1 from the Banana Genome Hub<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Droc, G. et al. The banana genome hub. Database 2013 (2013)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1894">45</a></sup> and <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> DPV01, <i>Oryza sativa</i> IRGSP-1.0, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> v3.1, <i>Zea mays</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> TAIR10 from Phytozome (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov</a>) or related databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 5</a>) and then blasted against the repeat-masked genome assembly of <i>M. itinerans</i> using TBLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Gertz, E. M., Yu, Y.-K., Agarwala, R., Schäffer, A. a. &amp; Altschul, S. F. Composition-based statistics and translated nucleotide searches: improving the TBLASTN module of BLAST. BMC Biol. 4, 41 (2006)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1928">46</a></sup> with percentage identity over 70% and coverage not less than 50%. Gene models were predicted based on these alignments using Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Birney, E. &amp; Durbin, R. Using GeneWise in the Drosophila annotation experiment. Genome Res. 10, 547–548 (2000)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1932">47</a></sup>. <i>De novo</i> gene predictions were performed using Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: A b initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34 (2006)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1939">48</a></sup> and GENSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1943">49</a></sup>. Both sets of genes were integrated using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Elsik, C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1947">50</a></sup>. The annotations of the final gene sets were obtained by searching against the protein database KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1952">51</a></sup>, SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1956">52</a></sup>, TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365–370 (2003)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1960">53</a></sup> using an e-value cut-off of 1e-5 and motifs and domains for the gene set were determined using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan--an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1964">54</a></sup>. Using CEGMA package, the completeness of the gene space for the draft genome assembly was evaluated with the highly conserved 248 core eukaryotic gene (CEGs).</p><p>For noncoding RNAs, tRNAs were identified using tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: A program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1971">55</a></sup>, snRNAs and miRNAs and rRNAs were obtained by searching the genome assembly against the Rfam database using InFERNAL with default parameters (<a href="http://infernal.janelia.org/">http://infernal.janelia.org/</a>). All the raw short reads, genome assembly were deposited in NBCI’s Sequence Read Archive (SRA) database with accession number PRJNA312694 and the gene annotation used for the analyses were provided to the Banana Genome Hub (banana-genome-hub.southgreen.fr/organism/Musa_itinerans).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome-wide duplications and estimation of the insertion times for LTR retrotransposons</h3><p>Using the web-based tool SynMap on the plant comparative genome platform CoGe (<a href="https://genomevolution.org/CoGe/">https://genomevolution.org/CoGe/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 53, 661–673 (2008)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d6416e1997">56</a></sup>, the syntenic regions between <i>M. itinerans</i> and <i>M. acuminata</i> were generated and scaffolds or contigs of <i>M. itinerans</i> were mapped or oriented on the 11 pseudo-chromosomes of <i>M. acuminata</i>. Reciprocal BLASTP between protein sequences of two <i>Musa</i> species with E-value cut-off of 1e-5, were implemented to identify orthologs between species and self BLASTP for both species were used to obtain paralogs within species. After removing the self-matches of the BLASTP results, the syntenic blocks (≥5 genes per block) were determined with the package MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: A toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, 1–11 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2017">25</a></sup>. The remaining aligned results were used to generate dot plots. Paralogous gene pairs in each block within species were used to infer genome duplication events. Meanwhile, orthologous gene pairs in each block between species provided some insights into the speciation events. The four-fold transversion (4DTv) ratio for each gene pair in the block was calculated on concatenated nucleotide alignments with HKY substitution models and the distributions of these 4DTv values were used to estimate the whole genome duplication events and speciation events.</p><p>The 5′- and 3′-LTR sequences of the full-length LTR retrotransposons were aligned and the <i>K</i> value (the average number of substitutions per aligned site) were calculated in the package MEGA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Tamura, K., Dudley, J., Nei, M. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA4: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis (MEGA) software version 4.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1596–1599 (2007)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2027">57</a></sup>. The insertion times were estimated using this formula: T = K/(2 × r), where r represents the average substitution rate and is estimated to be 1.30 × 10<sup>−8</sup> substitutions per site per year in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2033">39</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>The protein sequences of eight plant species including <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>, <i>Elaeis guineensis</i>, <i>M. acuminata,</i> and <i>M. balbisiana</i> were downloaded (sources details in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 5</a>). After filtering out protein sequences with amino acid length of less than 50, the longest protein sequence was chosen among spliced variants. All the eight protein datasets together with those of <i>M. itinerans</i> were clustered into paralogous and orthologous groups using the program OrthoMCL v1.4 with the inflation parameter of 1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2076">58</a></sup>. Protein sequences of each single-copy gene obtained from the nine species were aligned using MUSCLE v3.8.31<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: Multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2081">59</a></sup> and corresponding nucleotide sequences alignment of different genes were concatenated into one supergene. Using 4DTv sites of the supergene with the nucleotide substitution model HKY85 + gamma, a maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree of the nine species was constructed using the package PhyML with NNI (Nearest Neighbor Interchange) tree improvement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Guindon, S., Dufayard, J.-F., Lefort, V. &amp; Anisimova, M. New Alogrithms and Methods to Estimate Maximum- Likelihoods Phylogenies: Assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307–321 (2010)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2085">60</a></sup>. Bootstrap supports of the internal branches were evaluated by 1000 full-heuristic searches. Divergence time estimations between species were determined using MCMCtree in PAML 4.7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 555–556 (1997)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2089">61</a></sup> and the “correlated molecular clock” and “REV” substitution model were used in the approximate likelihood calculation. The MCMC process was run for 2,000,000 steps and sampled every 20,000 steps. A calibrated divergence time of 124~248.4 Ma between <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Sorghum</i> was used<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Clarke, J. T., Warnock, R. C. M. &amp; Donoghue, P. C. J. Establishing a time-scale for plant evolution. New Phytol. 192, 266–301 (2011)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2099">23</a></sup>. Gene family expansions and contractions of extant species and ancient lineages were estimated using CAFE v2.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269–1271 (2006)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2104">62</a></sup>. Using a random birth and death model, the gene birth (λ) and death (μ = −λ) rates were estimated across the species tree composed of <i>M. itinerans</i> and eight other species, using the maximum likelihood method and gene families with accelerated rate of expansion and contraction were determined with a threshold conditional P-value (P &lt; 0.05).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene family evolution</h3><p>Using HMMER V3 (<a href="http://hmmer.janelia.org/software">http://hmmer.janelia.org/software</a>), protein sequences of <i>M. acuminata, M. itinerans,</i> and <i>M. balbisiana</i> were aligned against the raw hidden Markov model (HMM) corresponding to the Pfam NBS (NB-ARC) family (PF00931, downloaded from website <a href="http://pfam.sanger.ac.uk/">http://pfam.sanger.ac.uk/</a>). Following the method described in D’ Hont <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2142">8</a></sup>, the hit NBS-domain sequences were aligned and used to construct a new NBS HMM profile using the module ‘hmmbuild’ . Using this <i>Musa</i> specific model, we identified NBS- candidate proteins in the three <i>Musa</i> species respectively. TIR and LRR domains of these NBS-encoding amino acid sequences were identified with Pfam_scan. Coiled-Coil (CC) motif was identified using MARCOIL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Delorenzi, M. &amp; Speed, T. An HMM model for coiled-coil domains and a comparison with PSSM-based predictions. Bioinformatics 18, 617–625 (2002)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2153">63</a></sup> with a threshold probability of 90 and double-checked using paircoil<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="McDonnell, A. V., Jiang, T., Keating, A. E. &amp; Berger, B. Paircoil2: Improved prediction of coiled coils from sequence. Bioinformatics 22, 356–358 (2006)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2157">64</a></sup>. Duplication modes of these NBS-encoding genes were identified in MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: A toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, 1–11 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2161">25</a></sup>.</p><p>Transcription factors’ gene families (TFs) of the <i>M. itinerans</i> were identified by two complementary approaches. First, protein sequences of <i>M. itinerans</i> were BLASTP against the TFs of five other species including <i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> (<a href="http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/">http://planttfdb.cbi.pku.edu.cn/</a>). Second, <i>de novo</i> predicted TFs were obtained using all the TFs of the other five species to TBLASTN against the genome of <i>M. itinerans</i> and corresponding gene models were predicted using Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Birney, E. &amp; Durbin, R. Using GeneWise in the Drosophila annotation experiment. Genome Res. 10, 547–548 (2000)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2203">47</a></sup>. Using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Elsik, C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2208">50</a></sup>, non-overlapping TFs of <i>M. itinerans</i> were obtained. The duplication history of the MYB gene family was inferred using the programme MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: A toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, 1–11 (2012)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2215">25</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Demographic history inference</h3><p>One individual from each of the three populations was sequenced in high depth (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep31586#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1</a>) and qualified reads from the short insert size library (500 bp or 180 bp) were realigned to the assembly with BWA with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2230">65</a></sup>. Consensus sequences were called using samtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2234">66</a></sup> and converted into the Fastq format using bcftools and vcfutils in the package pairwise sequentially Markovian coalescent model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Inference of human population history from individual whole-genome sequences. Nature 475, 493–496 (2011)." href="/articles/srep31586#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d6416e2238">38</a></sup> (PMSC). The consensus sequences were split into non-overlapping 100 bp bins marked as homozygous or heterozygous and these split sequences were used to reconstruct the demographic history with the PSMC model with the following parameters: “N30 -t15 -r3 -b -p “4 + 5 * 3 + 4”. The variance of the simulation results was assessed with 100 bootstrap replicates. Finally, the PSMC profiles were scaled using generation time (<i>g</i>) of one year and neutral mutation rate μ of 1.30 × 10<sup>−8</sup> substitution per site per year.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec17" data-title="Additional Information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec17-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec17">Additional Information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec17-content"><p><b>Accession codes:</b> The whole Genome assembly sequences and raw reads have been deposited in GenBank DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the BioProject ID PRJNA312694, with accession numbers SRR3180710, SRR3180652, SRR3180729, SRR3180738 and SRR3180744 for raw reads and LVTN00000000 for genome assembly and the version described in this paper is version LVTN01000000. The genome assembly and annotation files for <i>Musa itinerans</i> can also be accessed by The Banana Genome Hub (banana-genome-hub.southgreen.fr/organism/Musa_itinerans).</p><p><b>How to cite this article</b>: Wu, W. <i>et al.</i> Whole genome sequencing of a banana wild relative <i>Musa itinerans</i> provides insights into lineage-specific diversification of the <i>Musa</i> genus. <i>Sci. Rep.</i> <b>6</b>, 31586; doi: 10.1038/srep31586 (2016).</p></div></div></section>
